      <p><b>Finland</b> (<a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>: <i lang="fi">Suomi</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Finnish" title="Help:IPA/Finnish">[ˈsuo̯mi]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fi-suomi.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Fi-suomi.ogg" class="internal" title="Fi-suomi.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>: <i lang="sv">Finland</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Swedish" title="Help:IPA/Swedish">[ˈfɪ̌nland]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sv-Finland.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Sv-Finland.ogg" class="internal" title="Sv-Finland.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>, <small>Finland Swedish:&nbsp;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Swedish" title="Help:IPA/Swedish">[ˈfinlɑnd]</a></span>), officially the <b>Republic of Finland</b> (<a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>: <i lang="fi">Suomen tasavalta</i>, <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>: <i lang="sv">Republiken Finland</i> <span style="font-size:85%;">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Suomi_Finland.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Suomi_Finland.ogg" class="internal" title="Suomi Finland.ogg">listen to all</a></span></span>)</span>),<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[note 1]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Nordic country">Nordic country</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>. It shares land borders with <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> to the north, and is defined by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a> to the west, and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> across <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> to the south. Finland covers an area of 338,455 square kilometres (130,678&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), with a population of 5.5 million. <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a> is the country's capital and largest city, but together with the neighboring cities of <a href="/wiki/Espoo" title="Espoo">Espoo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kauniainen" title="Kauniainen">Kauniainen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vantaa" title="Vantaa">Vantaa</a>, it forms a larger <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">metropolitan area</a>. <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Native_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Native language">native language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">Finns</a>, is among the few <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic languages</a> in the world. The climate varies relative to latitude, from the southern <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a> to the northern <a href="/wiki/Boreal_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Boreal climate">boreal climate</a>. The land cover is primarily a <a href="/wiki/Boreal_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Boreal forest">boreal forest</a> <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biome</a>, with more than 180,000 recorded <a href="/wiki/List_of_lakes_of_Finland" title="List of lakes of Finland">lakes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thousand_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thousand-12">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Finland was inhabited around 9000&nbsp;BC after <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Last glacial period">the Last Glacial Period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> introduced several different ceramic styles and cultures. The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> were characterised by extensive contacts with other cultures in <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_region" title="Baltic region">Baltic region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> From the late 13th century, Finland gradually became an integral part of Sweden as a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a>. In 1809, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_War" title="Finnish War">Finnish War</a>, Finland was annexed by <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> as the autonomous <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">Grand Duchy of Finland</a>, during which <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Finnish_Art" title="Golden Age of Finnish Art">Finnish art flourished</a> and the idea of <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Finland" title="Independence of Finland">independence</a> began to take hold. In 1906, Finland became the first European state to grant <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage#Dates_by_country" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a>, and the first in the world to give all adult citizens the right to run for public office.<sup id="cite_ref-eduskunta_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eduskunta-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-full_suffrage_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-full_suffrage-16">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Nicholas II</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsar</a> of Russia, tried to <a href="/wiki/Russification_of_Finland" title="Russification of Finland">russify Finland</a> and terminate its political autonomy, but after the 1917 <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>, Finland <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Finnish Declaration of Independence">declared independence from Russia</a>. In 1918, the fledgling state was divided by the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Civil_War" title="Finnish Civil War">Finnish Civil War</a>. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Finland fought the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Continuation_War" title="Continuation War">Continuation War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Lapland_War" title="Lapland War">Lapland War</a>. After the wars, Finland lost parts of its territory, but maintained its independence. </p>
      <p>Finland largely remained an <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agrarian</a> country until the 1950s. After World War II, the country rapidly industrialised and developed an advanced economy, while building an extensive <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_model" title="Nordic model">Nordic model</a>, resulting in widespread prosperity and a high <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal) per capita">per capita income</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-imf2_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-imf2-17">[16]</a></sup> Finland joined the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in 1955 and adopted an official policy of neutrality. Finland joined the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> in 1969, the <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace" title="Partnership for Peace">NATO Partnership for Peace</a> in 1994,<sup id="cite_ref-nato.int_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nato.int-18">[17]</a></sup> the European Union in 1995, the <a href="/wiki/Euro-Atlantic_Partnership_Council" title="Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council">Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council</a> in 1997,<sup id="cite_ref-nato.int_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nato.int-18">[17]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a> at its inception in 1999. Finland is a top performer in numerous <a href="/wiki/International_rankings_of_Finland" title="International rankings of Finland">metrics of national performance</a>, including education, economic competitiveness, civil liberties, quality of life and human development.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Audit_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Audit-19">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> In 2015, Finland was ranked first in the World Human Capital<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index" title="Press Freedom Index">Press Freedom Index</a> and as the most stable country in the world during 2011–2016 in the <a href="/wiki/Fragile_States_Index" title="Fragile States Index">Fragile States Index</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> and second in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Gender_Gap_Report" title="Global Gender Gap Report">Global Gender Gap Report</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> It also ranked first on the <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report</a> report for 2018, 2019, 2020 and 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg/310px-Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="414" data-file-height="333"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hic_Fabricatur_Naves.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Finland on a medieval map, which is part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Carta_marina" title="Carta marina">Carta marina</a></i> (1539)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Finns#Etymology" title="Finns">Finns §&nbsp;Etymology</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The earliest written appearance of the name <i>Finland</i> is thought to be on three <a href="/wiki/Runestone" title="Runestone">runestones</a>. Two were found in the Swedish province of <a href="/wiki/Uppland" title="Uppland">Uppland</a> and have the inscription <i>finlonti</i> (<a href="/wiki/Runestone_U_582" title="Runestone U 582">U 582</a>). The third was found in <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>. It has the inscription <i>finlandi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Runestone_G_319" title="Runestone G 319">G 319</a>) and dates back to the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> The name can be assumed to be related to the tribe name <i><a href="/wiki/Finnish_tribes" title="Finnish tribes">Finns</a></i>, which is mentioned at first known time AD 98 (disputed meaning). </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suomi">Suomi</span></h3> 
      <p>The name <i>Suomi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> for '<i lang="fi" title="Finnish-language text">Finland</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>) has uncertain origins, but a common etymology with <i>saame</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sami_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami people">Sami</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_peoples" title="Finno-Ugric peoples">Finno-Ugric people</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">Lapland</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Tavastia_(historical_province)" title="Tavastia (historical province)">Häme</a></i> (a province in the inland) has been suggested (<a href="/wiki/Proto-Finnic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Finnic">Proto-Finnic</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Finnic/h%C3%A4m%C3%A4" class="extiw" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Finnic/hämä"><i>*hämä</i></a> from older <i>*šämä</i>, possibly loaned into Proto-Saami as <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Samic/s%C4%81m%C4%93" class="extiw" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Samic/sāmē"><i>*sāmē</i></a>), whose source could be the Proto-Baltic word <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Balto-Slavic/%C5%BAem%C4%93" class="extiw" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Balto-Slavic/źemē"><i>*źemē</i></a>, meaning '(low) land'.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> According to the hypothesis, <i>*sāmē</i> – or <i>*šämä</i> directly – was loaned back into Baltic as <i>*sāma-</i> (compare Latvian <i>sāms</i> 'Finn, <a href="/wiki/Saaremaa" title="Saaremaa">Öselian</a>'), from which Northern Finnic reborrowed it (perhaps via a Germanic intermediate <i>*sōma-</i>) as <i>*sōma-</i> &gt; <i>*sōme-</i> 'Finland'.<sup id="cite_ref-deSmit_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deSmit-30">[29]</a></sup> In addition to the close relatives of Finnish (the <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic languages</a>), this name is also used in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic languages</a> <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a> (<i>soms</i>, <i>Somija</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a> (<i>suomis</i>, <i>Suomija</i>), although these are evidently later borrowings. An alternative hypothesis by Petri Kallio suggests the Proto-Indo-European word <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/%C7%B5%CA%B0m%E1%B9%93" class="extiw" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/ǵʰmṓ">*<i>(dʰ)ǵʰm-on-</i></a> 'human' (cf. Gothic <i>guma</i>, Latin <i>homo</i>), being borrowed into Uralic as *<i>ćoma</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-deSmit_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deSmit-30">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It has been suggested that the Finnish word <i>Suomi</i> is first attested the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Frankish_Annals" title="Royal Frankish Annals">Royal Frankish Annals</a> annal for 811, which mentions a person called <i>Suomi</i> among the Danish delegation at a peace treaty with the Franks.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> If so, it is also the earliest evidence for the change from the proto-Finnic <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthong</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/oː/</span> to the Finnish <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthong</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uo/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> However, some historical linguists view this interpretation of the name as unlikely, supposing another etymology or that the spelling originated as a scribal error (in which case the sound-change <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/oː/</span> &gt; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uo/</span> could have happened much later).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept">Concept</span></h3> 
      <p>In the earliest historical sources, from the 12th and 13th centuries, the term Finland refers to the coastal region around <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a> from <a href="/wiki/Perni%C3%B6" title="Perniö">Perniö</a> to <a href="/wiki/Uusikaupunki" title="Uusikaupunki">Uusikaupunki</a>. This region later became known as <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Finland" title="Southwest Finland">Finland Proper</a> in distinction from the country name Finland. Finland became a common name for the whole country in a centuries-long process that started when the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> established a missionary diocese in <a href="/wiki/Nousiainen" title="Nousiainen">Nousiainen</a> in the northern part of the province of Suomi possibly sometime in the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-35">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The devastation of <a href="/wiki/Finland_during_the_Great_Northern_War" title="Finland during the Great Northern War">Finland during the Great Northern War</a> (1714–1721) and during the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Swedish_War_(1741%E2%80%931743)" title="Russo-Swedish War (1741–1743)">Russo-Swedish War (1741–1743)</a> caused Sweden to begin carrying out major efforts to defend its eastern half from Russia. These 18th-century experiences created a sense of a shared destiny that when put in conjunction with the unique Finnish language, led to the adoption of an expanded concept of Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-UppslagsFi_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UppslagsFi-36">[35]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Finland" title="History of Finland">History of Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">Åland Islands</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Finland#Prehistory" title="History of Finland">History of Finland §&nbsp;Prehistory</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg/220px-Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg/330px-Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg/440px-Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2855" data-file-height="1750"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> dwelling from <a href="/wiki/Kierikki" title="Kierikki">Kierikki</a>, Oulu
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>If the <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archeological</a> finds from <a href="/wiki/Wolf_Cave" title="Wolf Cave">Wolf Cave</a> are the result of <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals'</a> activities, the first people inhabited Finland approximately 120,000–130,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> The area that is now Finland was settled in, at the latest, around 8,500 BC during the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> towards the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">artifacts</a> the first settlers left behind present characteristics that are shared with those found in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, Russia, and Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-38">[37]</a></sup> The earliest people were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>, using stone tools.<sup id="cite_ref-VF-Pre_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VF-Pre-39">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first pottery appeared in 5200&nbsp;BC, when the Comb Ceramic culture was introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-Hist-Fin-Geo_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hist-Fin-Geo-40">[39]</a></sup> The arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Corded_Ware_culture" title="Corded Ware culture">Corded Ware culture</a> in Southern coastal Finland between 3000 and 2500 BC may have coincided with the start of agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-Virt-Mino_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Virt-Mino-41">[40]</a></sup> Even with the introduction of agriculture, hunting and fishing continued to be important parts of the subsistence economy. </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> permanent all-year-round cultivation and <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a> spread, but the cold climate phase slowed the change.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42">[41]</a></sup> Cultures in Finland shared common features in pottery and also axes had similarities but local features existed. <a href="/wiki/Seima-Turbino_phenomenon" title="Seima-Turbino phenomenon">Seima-Turbino-phenomenon</a> brought first bronze artifacts to the region and possibly also the <a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages" title="Finno-Ugric languages">Finno-Ugric-Languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> Commercial contacts that had so far mostly been to <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> started to extend to Scandinavia. Domestic manufacture of bronze artifacts started 1300 BC with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Maaninka-type_bronze_axe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Maaninka-type bronze axe (page does not exist)">Maaninka-type bronze axes</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&nbsp;[<a href="https://fi.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maaningan_kirves" class="extiw" title="fi:Maaningan kirves">fi</a>]</span>. Bronze was imported from <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Volga River">Volga</a> region and from Southern Scandinavia.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg/220px-Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg/330px-Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg/440px-Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1476" data-file-height="946"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a> in 814 AD
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> population grew especially in Häme and Savo regions. Finland proper was the most densely populated area. Cultural contacts to the Baltics and Scandinavia became more frequent. Commercial contacts in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> region grew and extended during the 8th and 9th centuries. </p>
      <p>Main exports from Finland were furs, slaves, <a href="/wiki/Castoreum" title="Castoreum">castoreum</a>, and falcons to European courts. Imports included silk and other fabrics, jewelry, <a href="/wiki/Ulfberht_swords" title="Ulfberht swords">Ulfberht swords</a>, and, in lesser extent, glass. Production of iron started approximately in 500 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At the end of the 9th century, indigenous artifact culture, especially women's jewelry and weapons, had more common local features than ever before. This has been interpreted to be expressing common Finnish identity which was born from an image of common origin.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg/220px-Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg/330px-Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg/440px-Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="614" data-file-height="444"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Late <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> swords found in Finland
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>An early form of <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic languages</a> spread to the Baltic Sea region approximately 1900 BC with the <a href="/wiki/Seima-Turbino_phenomenon" title="Seima-Turbino phenomenon">Seima-Turbino-phenomenon</a>. Common Finnic language was spoken around <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a> 2000 years ago. The dialects from which the modern-day Finnish language was developed came into existence during the Iron Age.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> Although distantly related, the <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami people">Sami</a> retained the hunter-gatherer lifestyle longer than the Finns. The Sami cultural identity and the <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">Sami language</a> have survived in Lapland, the northernmost province, but the Sami have been displaced or assimilated elsewhere. </p>
      <p>The 12th and 13th centuries were a violent time in the northern Baltic Sea. The <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Crusade" title="Livonian Crusade">Livonian Crusade</a> was ongoing and the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_tribes" title="Finnish tribes">Finnish tribes</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Tavastians" title="Tavastians">Tavastians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karelia_(historical_province_of_Finland)" title="Karelia (historical province of Finland)">Karelians</a> were in <a href="/wiki/Early_Finnish_wars" title="Early Finnish wars">frequent conflicts</a> with <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Novgorod" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Novgorod">Novgorod</a> and with each other. Also, during the 12th and 13th centuries several crusades from the Catholic realms of the Baltic Sea area were made against the Finnish tribes. According to historical sources, <a href="/wiki/Danes" title="Danes">Danes</a> waged at least three crusades to Finland, in 1187 or slightly earlier,<sup id="cite_ref-:3_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-48">[47]</a></sup> in 1191 and in 1202,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Swedes" title="Swedes">Swedes</a>, possibly the so-called <a href="/wiki/Second_Swedish_Crusade" title="Second Swedish Crusade">second crusade to Finland</a>, in 1249 against <a href="/wiki/Tavastians" title="Tavastians">Tavastians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Third_Swedish_Crusade" title="Third Swedish Crusade">third crusade to Finland</a> in 1293 against the Karelians. The so-called <a href="/wiki/First_Swedish_Crusade" title="First Swedish Crusade">first crusade to Finland</a>, possibly in 1155, is most likely an unreal event. Also, it is possible that Germans made violent conversion of Finnish pagans in the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> According to a papal letter from 1241, the king of Norway was also fighting against "nearby pagans" at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swedish_era">Swedish era</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Finland_under_Swedish_rule" title="Finland under Swedish rule">Finland under Swedish rule</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Swedish_colonisation_of_Finland" title="Swedish colonisation of Finland">Swedish colonisation of Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Swedish_Empire_(1560-1815)_en2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/220px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/330px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/440px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png 2x" data-file-width="1802" data-file-height="2213"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Swedish_Empire_(1560-1815)_en2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Empire" title="Swedish Empire">Swedish Empire</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Roskilde" title="Treaty of Roskilde">Treaty of Roskilde</a> of 1658.
         <br>Dark green: <a href="/wiki/Sweden_proper" title="Sweden proper">Sweden proper</a>, as represented in the <a href="/wiki/Riksdag_of_the_Estates" title="Riksdag of the Estates">Riksdag of the Estates</a>. Other greens: <a href="/wiki/Dominions_of_Sweden" title="Dominions of Sweden">Swedish dominions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Possessions_of_Sweden" title="Possessions of Sweden">possessions</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>As a result of the crusades and the colonisation of some Finnish coastal areas with Christian <a href="/wiki/Swedish-speaking_population_of_Finland" title="Swedish-speaking population of Finland">Swedish population</a> during the Middle Ages,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> including the old capital <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a>, Finland gradually became part of the kingdom of Sweden and the sphere of influence of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>. Due to the Swedish conquest, the Finnish upper class lost its position and lands to the new Swedish and German nobility and to the Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> In Sweden even in the 17th and 18th centuries, it was clear that Finland was a conquered country and its inhabitants could be treated arbitrarily. Swedish kings visited Finland rarely and in Swedish contemporary texts Finns were portrayed to be primitive and their language inferior.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Finland_Swedish" title="Finland Swedish">Swedish</a> became the dominant language of the nobility, administration, and education; <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> was chiefly a language for the <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasantry</a>, clergy, and local <a href="/wiki/Court" title="Court">courts</a> in predominantly Finnish-speaking areas. During the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">Finns</a> gradually converted to <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Mikael_Agricola" title="Mikael Agricola">Mikael Agricola</a> published the first written works in Finnish, and Finland's current capital city, <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, was founded by <a href="/wiki/Gustav_I_of_Sweden" title="Gustav I of Sweden">Gustav I of Sweden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> The first university in Finland, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Turku" title="Royal Academy of Turku">Royal Academy of Turku</a>, was established in 1640. The Finns reaped a reputation in the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> (1618–1648) as a well-trained <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalrymen</a> called "<a href="/wiki/Hakkapeliitta" title="Hakkapeliitta">Hakkapeliitta</a>", that division excelled in sudden and savage attacks, <a href="/wiki/Raid_(military)" title="Raid (military)">raiding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Gustavus_Adolphus_of_Sweden" title="Gustavus Adolphus of Sweden">King Gustavus Adolphus</a> took advantage of in his significant battles, like in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Breitenfeld_(1631)" title="Battle of Breitenfeld (1631)">Battle of Breitenfeld</a> (1631) and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Rain" title="Battle of Rain">Battle of Rain</a> (1632).<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Finland suffered a severe <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_Finland_(1695%E2%80%931697)" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Famine of Finland (1695–1697)">famine in 1696–1697</a>, during which about one third of the Finnish population died,<sup id="cite_ref-empire_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-empire-59">[58]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/The_plague_during_the_Great_Northern_War" class="mw-redirect" title="The plague during the Great Northern War">devastating plague a few years later</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Suomenlinna.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Suomenlinna.jpg/220px-Suomenlinna.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Suomenlinna.jpg/330px-Suomenlinna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Suomenlinna.jpg/440px-Suomenlinna.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1810" data-file-height="1360"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Suomenlinna.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Now lying within Helsinki, <a href="/wiki/Suomenlinna" title="Suomenlinna">Suomenlinna</a> is a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> consisting of an inhabited 18th-century sea fortress built on six islands. It is one of Finland's most popular tourist attractions.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the 18th century, wars between Sweden and Russia twice led to the occupation of Finland by Russian forces, times known to the Finns as the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Wrath" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Wrath">Greater Wrath</a> (1714–1721) and the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Swedish_War_(1741%E2%80%931743)" title="Russo-Swedish War (1741–1743)">Lesser Wrath</a> (1742–1743).<sup id="cite_ref-empire_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-empire-59">[58]</a></sup> It is estimated that almost an entire generation of young men was lost during the Great Wrath, due mainly to the destruction of homes and farms, and to the burning of Helsinki.<sup id="cite_ref-Nordstrom,_Scandinavia_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nordstrom,_Scandinavia-60">[59]</a></sup> By this time Finland was the predominant term for the whole area from the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a> to the Russian border.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Two Russo-Swedish wars in twenty-five years served as reminders to the Finnish people of the precarious position between Sweden and Russia. An increasingly vocal elite in Finland soon determined that Finnish ties with Sweden were becoming too costly, and following <a href="/wiki/Russo-Swedish_War_(1788%E2%80%931790)" title="Russo-Swedish War (1788–1790)">Russo-Swedish War</a> (1788–1790), the Finnish elite's desire to break with Sweden only heightened.<sup id="cite_ref-a1_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a1-61">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Even before the war there were conspiring politicians, among them <a href="/wiki/Georg_Magnus_Sprengtporten" title="Georg Magnus Sprengtporten">Col G. M. Sprengtporten</a>, who had supported <a href="/wiki/Gustav_III_of_Sweden#Coup_d'état" class="mw-redirect" title="Gustav III of Sweden">Gustav III's coup in 1772</a>. Sprengporten fell out with the king and resigned his commission in 1777. In the following decade he tried to secure Russian support for an autonomous Finland, and later became an adviser to Catherine II.<sup id="cite_ref-a1_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a1-61">[60]</a></sup> In the spirit of the notion of <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Ivar_Arwidsson" title="Adolf Ivar Arwidsson">Adolf Ivar Arwidsson</a> (1791–1858), "we are not Swedes, we do not want to become Russians, let us therefore be Finns", the Finnish national identity started to become established.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Notwithstanding the efforts of Finland's elite and nobility to break ties with Sweden, there was no genuine independence movement in Finland until the early 20th century. As a matter of fact, at this time the Finnish peasantry was outraged by the actions of their elite and almost exclusively supported Gustav's actions against the conspirators. (The High Court of Turku condemned Sprengtporten as a traitor c. 1793.)<sup id="cite_ref-a1_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a1-61">[60]</a></sup> The Swedish era ended in the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_War" title="Finnish War">Finnish War</a> in 1809. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_era">Russian era</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">Grand Duchy of Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Porvoo" title="Diet of Porvoo">Diet of Porvoo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland%27s_language_strife" title="Finland's language strife">Finland's language strife</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russification_of_Finland" title="Russification of Finland">Russification of Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg/220px-Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg/330px-Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg/440px-Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1494"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><i>Pioneers in Karelia</i> (1900) by <a href="/wiki/Pekka_Halonen" title="Pekka Halonen">Pekka Halonen</a><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 29 March 1809, having been taken over by the armies of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Alexander I of Russia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_War" title="Finnish War">Finnish War</a>, Finland became an autonomous <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">Grand Duchy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> with the recognition given at <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Porvoo" title="Diet of Porvoo">the Diet</a> held in <a href="/wiki/Porvoo" title="Porvoo">Porvoo</a>. This situation lasted until the end of 1917. In 1811, Alexander I incorporated the Russian <a href="/wiki/Vyborg_Governorate" title="Vyborg Governorate">Vyborg province</a> into the Grand Duchy of Finland. In 1854, Finland became involved in Russia's involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, when the British and French navies bombed the Finnish coast and Åland during the so-called <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_War" title="Åland War">Åland War</a>. During the Russian era, the Finnish language began to gain recognition. From the 1860s onwards, a strong Finnish <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_nationalism" title="Ethnic nationalism">nationalist movement</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Fennoman_movement" title="Fennoman movement">Fennoman movement</a> grew, and one of its most prominent leading figures of the movement was the philosopher <a href="/wiki/J._V._Snellman" class="mw-redirect" title="J. V. Snellman">J. V. Snellman</a>, who was strictly inclined to <a href="/wiki/Hegelianism" title="Hegelianism">Hegel's idealism</a>, and who pushed for the stabilization of the status of the Finnish language and its own currency, the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_markka" title="Finnish markka">Finnish markka</a>, in the Grand Duchy of Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Milestones included the publication of what would become Finland's <a href="/wiki/National_epic" title="National epic">national epic</a> – the <i><a href="/wiki/Kalevala" title="Kalevala">Kalevala</a></i> – in 1835, and the Finnish language's achieving equal legal status with Swedish in 1892. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Finnish_famine_of_1866%E2%80%931868" title="Finnish famine of 1866–1868">Finnish famine of 1866–1868</a> killed approximately 15% of the population, making it one of the worst <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famines</a> in European history. The famine led the Russian Empire to ease financial regulations, and investment rose in following decades. Economic and political development was rapid.<sup id="cite_ref-equity_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-equity-66">[65]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) per capita was still half of that of the United States and a third of that of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-equity_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-equity-66">[65]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1906, <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a> was adopted in the Grand Duchy of Finland. However, the relationship between the Grand Duchy and the Russian Empire soured when the Russian government made moves to restrict Finnish <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous area">autonomy</a>. For example, the universal suffrage was, in practice, virtually meaningless, since the tsar did not have to approve any of the laws adopted by the Finnish parliament. Desire for independence gained ground, first among radical liberals<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> and socialists. The case is known as the "<a href="/wiki/Russification_of_Finland" title="Russification of Finland">Russification of Finland</a>", driven by the last tsar of Russian Empire, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Nicholas II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war_and_early_independence">Civil war and early independence</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Finland" title="Independence of Finland">Independence of Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Socialist_Workers%27_Republic" title="Finnish Socialist Workers' Republic">Finnish Socialist Workers' Republic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Civil_War" title="Finnish Civil War">Finnish Civil War</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Firing_squad_in_Lankipohja.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Firing_squad_in_Lankipohja.jpg/220px-Firing_squad_in_Lankipohja.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Firing_squad_in_Lankipohja.jpg/330px-Firing_squad_in_Lankipohja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Firing_squad_in_Lankipohja.jpg/440px-Firing_squad_in_Lankipohja.jpg 2x" data-file-width="528" data-file-height="347"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Firing_squad_in_Lankipohja.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/White_Guard_(Finland)" title="White Guard (Finland)">White firing squad</a> executing <a href="/wiki/Red_Guard_(Finland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Guard (Finland)">Red soldiers</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_L%C3%A4nkipohja" title="Battle of Länkipohja">Battle of Länkipohja</a> (1918)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the 1917 <a href="/wiki/February_Revolution" title="February Revolution">February Revolution</a>, the position of Finland as part of the Russian Empire was questioned, mainly by <a href="/wiki/Russian_Social_Democratic_Labour_Party" title="Russian Social Democratic Labour Party">Social Democrats</a>. Since the head of state was the <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">tsar</a> of Russia, it was not clear who the chief executive of Finland was after the revolution. The Parliament, controlled by social democrats, passed the so-called <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Finland#Power_act" title="Independence of Finland">Power Act</a> to give the highest authority to the Parliament. This was rejected by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Provisional_Government" title="Russian Provisional Government">Russian Provisional Government</a> which decided to dissolve the Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>New elections were conducted, in which right-wing parties won with a slim majority. Some social democrats refused to accept the result and still claimed that the dissolution of the parliament (and thus the ensuing elections) were extralegal. The two nearly equally powerful political blocs, the right-wing parties and the social democratic party, were highly antagonized. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a> in Russia changed the geopolitical situation once more. Suddenly, the right-wing parties in Finland started to reconsider their decision to block the transfer of highest executive power from the Russian government to Finland, as the <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a> took power in Russia. Rather than acknowledge the authority of the Power Act of a few months earlier, the right-wing government, led by <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Finland" title="Prime Minister of Finland">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/P._E._Svinhufvud" class="mw-redirect" title="P. E. Svinhufvud">P. E. Svinhufvud</a>, presented <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Finnish Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> on 4 December 1917, which was officially approved two days later, on 6 December, by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Finland" title="Parliament of Finland">Finnish Parliament</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic</a> (RSFSR), led by <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>, recognized independence on 4 January 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:The_victory_parade_of_the_White_Army_1918.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/The_victory_parade_of_the_White_Army_1918.jpg/220px-The_victory_parade_of_the_White_Army_1918.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/The_victory_parade_of_the_White_Army_1918.jpg/330px-The_victory_parade_of_the_White_Army_1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/The_victory_parade_of_the_White_Army_1918.jpg/440px-The_victory_parade_of_the_White_Army_1918.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3344" data-file-height="2085"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:The_victory_parade_of_the_White_Army_1918.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Finnish military leader and statesman <a href="/wiki/C._G._E._Mannerheim" class="mw-redirect" title="C. G. E. Mannerheim">C. G. E. Mannerheim</a> as <a href="/wiki/General_officer" title="General officer">general officer</a> leading the <a href="/wiki/White_Victory_Parade" class="mw-redirect" title="White Victory Parade">White Victory Parade</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Civil_War" title="Finnish Civil War">Finnish Civil War</a> in Helsinki, 1918
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 27 January 1918, the official opening shots of the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Civil_War" title="Finnish Civil War">civil war</a> were fired in two simultaneous events: on the one hand the government's beginning to disarm the Russian forces in <a href="/wiki/Ostrobothnia_(historical_province)" title="Ostrobothnia (historical province)">Pohjanmaa</a>, and on the other, a coup launched by the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Finland" title="Social Democratic Party of Finland">Social Democratic Party</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="No mention of coup in the section The Finnish Civil War, which should cover this. The actions by the reds are not characterized. (December 2010)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> The latter gained control of southern Finland and Helsinki, but the White government continued in exile from <a href="/wiki/Vaasa" title="Vaasa">Vaasa</a>. This sparked the brief but bitter civil war. The <a href="/wiki/White_Guard_(Finland)" title="White Guard (Finland)">Whites</a>, who were supported by <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Imperial Germany</a>, prevailed over the <a href="/wiki/Red_Guards_(Finland)" title="Red Guards (Finland)">Reds</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> which were guided by <a href="/wiki/Kullervo_Manner" title="Kullervo Manner">Kullervo Manner</a>'s desire to make the newly independent country a <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Socialist_Workers%27_Republic" title="Finnish Socialist Workers' Republic">Finnish Socialist Workers' Republic</a> (also known as "Red Finland") and part of the RSFSR.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> After the war, tens of thousands of Reds and suspected sympathizers were interned in camps, where thousands were executed or died from malnutrition and disease. Deep social and political enmity was sown between the Reds and Whites and would last until the <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a> and beyond. Even nowadays, the civil war remains a sensitive topic.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> The civil war and the 1918–1920 activist expeditions called "<a href="/wiki/Heimosodat" title="Heimosodat">Kinship Wars</a>" into Soviet Russia strained Eastern relations. At that time, the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Greater_Finland" title="Greater Finland">Greater Finland</a> also emerged for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Juho_kusti_paasikivi_and_Pehr_Evind_Svinhufvud_1918.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Juho_kusti_paasikivi_and_Pehr_Evind_Svinhufvud_1918.jpg/220px-Juho_kusti_paasikivi_and_Pehr_Evind_Svinhufvud_1918.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Juho_kusti_paasikivi_and_Pehr_Evind_Svinhufvud_1918.jpg/330px-Juho_kusti_paasikivi_and_Pehr_Evind_Svinhufvud_1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Juho_kusti_paasikivi_and_Pehr_Evind_Svinhufvud_1918.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="260"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Juho_kusti_paasikivi_and_Pehr_Evind_Svinhufvud_1918.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Juho_Kusti_Paasikivi" title="Juho Kusti Paasikivi">J. K. Paasikivi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pehr_Evind_Svinhufvud" title="Pehr Evind Svinhufvud">P. E. Svinhufvud</a>, both at the time future <a href="/wiki/President_of_Finland" title="President of Finland">presidents of the Republic of Finland</a>, discuss the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Finland_(1918)" title="Kingdom of Finland (1918)">Finnish monarchy project</a> in 1918.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Finland_(1918)" title="Kingdom of Finland (1918)">a brief experimentation with monarchy</a>, when an attempt to make <a href="/wiki/Prince_Frederick_Charles_of_Hesse" title="Prince Frederick Charles of Hesse">Prince Frederick Charles of Hesse</a> King of Finland proved to be a poor success, Finland became a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidential republic">presidential republic</a>, with <a href="/wiki/K._J._St%C3%A5hlberg" class="mw-redirect" title="K. J. Ståhlberg">K. J. Ståhlberg</a> elected as its first president in 1919. As a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal nationalist">liberal nationalist</a> and with a legal background, Ståhlberg anchored the state in <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>, guarded the fragile shoot of the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>, and embarked on internal reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Finland was also one of the first European countries to strongly aim for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">equality for women</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Miina_Sillanp%C3%A4%C3%A4" title="Miina Sillanpää">Miina Sillanpää</a> serving in <a href="/wiki/Tanner_Cabinet" title="Tanner Cabinet">Väinö Tanner's cabinet</a> as the first female minister in Finnish history in 1926–1927.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> The Finnish–Russian border was defined in 1920 by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tartu_(Finland%E2%80%93Russia)" title="Treaty of Tartu (Finland–Russia)">Treaty of Tartu</a>, largely following the historic border but granting <a href="/wiki/Pechengsky_District" title="Pechengsky District">Pechenga</a> (<a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>: <i lang="fi">Petsamo</i>) and its <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a> harbour to Finland. Finnish democracy did not experience any Soviet coup attempts and likewise survived the anti-communist <a href="/wiki/Lapua_Movement" title="Lapua Movement">Lapua Movement</a>. Nevertheless, the relationship between Finland and the Soviet Union remained tense. Army officers were trained in France, and relations with Western Europe and Sweden were strengthened. </p>
      <p>In 1917, the population was three million. Credit-based <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a> was enacted after the civil war, increasing the proportion of the capital-owning population.<sup id="cite_ref-equity_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-equity-66">[65]</a></sup> About 70% of workers were occupied in agriculture and 10% in industry.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> The largest export markets were the United Kingdom and Germany. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_and_after">World War II and after</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Finland_during_World_War_II" title="Finland during World War II">Finland during World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finno-Soviet_Treaty_of_1948" title="Finno-Soviet Treaty of 1948">Finno-Soviet Treaty of 1948</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finlandization" title="Finlandization">Finlandization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Early_1990s_depression_in_Finland" title="Early 1990s depression in Finland">Early 1990s depression in Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png/220px-Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png/330px-Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png/440px-Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png 2x" data-file-width="619" data-file-height="800"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Areas ceded by Finland to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Porkkala" class="mw-redirect" title="Porkkala">Porkkala</a> land lease was returned to Finland in 1956.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Finland fought the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a> of 1939–1940 after the Soviet Union attacked Finland and in the <a href="/wiki/Continuation_War" title="Continuation War">Continuation War</a> of 1941–1944, following <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a>, when Finland aligned with Germany following Germany's invasion of the Soviet Union. For 872 days, the German army, aided indirectly by Finnish forces, <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Leningrad" title="Siege of Leningrad">besieged Leningrad</a>, the USSR's second-largest city.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> After Finnish resistance to <a href="/wiki/Vyborg%E2%80%93Petrozavodsk_offensive" title="Vyborg–Petrozavodsk offensive">a major Soviet offensive</a> in June and July 1944 led to a standstill, the two sides reached an armistice. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Lapland_War" title="Lapland War">Lapland War</a> of 1944–1945, when Finland fought retreating German forces in northern Finland. Perhaps the most famous war heroes during the aforementioned wars were <a href="/wiki/Simo_H%C3%A4yh%C3%A4" title="Simo Häyhä">Simo Häyhä</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aarne_Juutilainen" title="Aarne Juutilainen">Aarne Juutilainen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lauri_T%C3%B6rni" title="Lauri Törni">Lauri Törni</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The treaties signed with the Soviet Union in 1947 and 1948 included Finnish obligations, restraints, and reparations, as well as further Finnish territorial concessions in addition to those in the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Peace_Treaty" title="Moscow Peace Treaty">Moscow Peace Treaty</a> of 1940. As a result of the two wars, Finland ceded the <a href="/wiki/Pechengsky_District" title="Pechengsky District">Petsamo</a>, along with parts of <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Karelia" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnish Karelia">Finnish Karelia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salla" title="Salla">Salla</a>. This amounted to 10% of Finland's land area and 20% of its industrial capacity, including the ports of <a href="/wiki/Vyborg" title="Vyborg">Vyborg</a> (Viipuri) and the ice-free <a href="/wiki/Liinakhamari" title="Liinakhamari">Liinakhamari</a> (Liinahamari). Almost the whole Finnish population, some 400,000 people, <a href="/wiki/Evacuation_of_Finnish_Karelia" title="Evacuation of Finnish Karelia">fled these areas</a>. The former Finnish territory now constitutes part of Russia's <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Karelia" title="Republic of Karelia">Republic of Karelia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leningrad_Oblast" title="Leningrad Oblast">Leningrad Oblast</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Murmansk_Oblast" title="Murmansk Oblast">Murmansk Oblast</a>. Finland was never occupied by Soviet forces and it retained its independence, but at a loss of about 97,000 soldiers. The <a href="/wiki/Finnish_war_reparations_to_the_Soviet_Union" title="Finnish war reparations to the Soviet Union">war reparations demanded by the Soviet Union</a> amounted to $300 million (5,516 million in 2019). </p>
      <p>Finland rejected <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall aid</a>, in apparent <a href="/wiki/Finlandization" title="Finlandization">deference to Soviet desires</a>. However, in the hope of preserving Finland's independence, the United States provided secret development aid and helped the Social Democratic Party.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Establishing trade with the Western powers, such as the United Kingdom, and paying reparations to the Soviet Union produced a transformation of Finland from a primarily <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Agrarian economy">agrarian economy</a> to an industrialised one. <a href="/wiki/Valmet" title="Valmet">Valmet</a> was founded to create materials for war reparations. After the reparations had been paid off, Finland continued to trade with the Soviet Union in the framework of <a href="/wiki/Bilateral_trade" title="Bilateral trade">bilateral trade</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Urho-Kekkonen-1975.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Urho-Kekkonen-1975.jpg/220px-Urho-Kekkonen-1975.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Urho-Kekkonen-1975.jpg/330px-Urho-Kekkonen-1975.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Urho-Kekkonen-1975.jpg/440px-Urho-Kekkonen-1975.jpg 2x" data-file-width="882" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Urho-Kekkonen-1975.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Urho_Kekkonen" title="Urho Kekkonen">Urho Kekkonen</a>, the eighth president of Finland (1956–1982)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1950, 46% of Finnish workers worked in agriculture and a third lived in urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-populationdevelopment_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-populationdevelopment-86">[85]</a></sup> The new jobs in manufacturing, services, and trade quickly attracted people to the towns. The average number of births per woman declined from a <a href="/wiki/Baby_boom" title="Baby boom">baby boom</a> peak of 3.5 in 1947 to 1.5 in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-populationdevelopment_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-populationdevelopment-86">[85]</a></sup> When baby-boomers entered the workforce, the economy did not generate jobs quickly enough, and hundreds of thousands emigrated to the more industrialized Sweden, with emigration peaking in 1969 and 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-populationdevelopment_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-populationdevelopment-86">[85]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1952_Summer_Olympics" title="1952 Summer Olympics">1952 Summer Olympics</a> brought international visitors. Finland took part in trade liberalization in the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> and the <a href="/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Tariffs_and_Trade" title="General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade">General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade</a>. </p>
      <p>Officially claiming to be <a href="/wiki/Neutral_country" title="Neutral country">neutral</a>, Finland lay in the grey zone between the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western countries</a> and the Soviet bloc. The <a href="/wiki/Finno-Soviet_Treaty_of_1948" title="Finno-Soviet Treaty of 1948">YYA Treaty</a> (Finno-Soviet Pact of Friendship, Cooperation and Mutual Assistance) gave the Soviet Union some leverage in Finnish domestic politics. This was extensively exploited by president <a href="/wiki/Urho_Kekkonen" title="Urho Kekkonen">Urho Kekkonen</a> against his opponents. He maintained an effective monopoly on Soviet relations from 1956 on, which was crucial for his continued popularity. In politics, there was a tendency to avoid any policies and statements that could be interpreted as anti-Soviet. This phenomenon was given the name "<a href="/wiki/Finlandization" title="Finlandization">Finlandization</a>" by the West German press. During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, Finland also developed into one of the centers of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_espionage" title="Cold War espionage">East-West espionage</a>, in which both the <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> and the <a href="/wiki/CIA" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA">CIA</a> played their parts.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> The 1949 established <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Security_Intelligence_Service" title="Finnish Security Intelligence Service">Finnish Security Intelligence Service</a> (<i>SUPO, Suojelupoliisi</i>), an operational <a href="/wiki/Security_agency" title="Security agency">security</a> authority and a police unit under the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_the_Interior_(Finland)" title="Ministry of the Interior (Finland)">Interior Ministry</a>, whose core areas of activity are <a href="/wiki/Counter-Intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter-Intelligence">counter-Intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Counter-terrorism" title="Counter-terrorism">counter-terrorism</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> also participated in this activity in some places.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Despite close relations with the Soviet Union, Finland maintained a market economy. Various industries benefited from <a href="/wiki/Bilateral_trade" title="Bilateral trade">trade privileges</a> with the Soviets, which explains the widespread support that pro-Soviet policies enjoyed among business interests in Finland. Economic growth was rapid in the postwar era, and by 1975 Finland's GDP per capita was the 15th-highest in the world. In the 1970s and 1980s, Finland built one of the most extensive <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare states</a> in the world. Finland negotiated with the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC, a predecessor of the European Union) a treaty that mostly abolished customs duties towards the EEC starting from 1977, although Finland did not fully join. In 1981, President Urho Kekkonen's failing health forced him to retire after holding office for 25 years. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_(081).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/220px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/330px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/440px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="521" data-file-height="310"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_(081).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Finland joined the European Union in 1995 and signed the <a href="/wiki/Lisbon_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Lisbon Treaty">Lisbon Treaty</a> in 2007.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Finland reacted cautiously to the collapse of the Soviet Union, but swiftly began increasing integration with the West. On 21 September 1990, Finland unilaterally declared the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris Peace Treaty">Paris Peace Treaty</a> obsolete, following the German reunification decision nine days earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Miscalculated macroeconomic decisions, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_banking_crisis_of_1990s" title="Finnish banking crisis of 1990s">a banking crisis</a>, the collapse of its largest trading partner (the Soviet Union), and a global economic downturn caused a deep <a href="/wiki/Early_1990s_depression_in_Finland" title="Early 1990s depression in Finland">early 1990s recession in Finland</a>. The depression bottomed out in 1993, and Finland saw steady economic growth for more than ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> Like other Nordic countries, Finland decentralised its economy since the late 1980s. Financial and product market regulation were loosened. Some state enterprises have been privatized and there have been some modest tax cuts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Finland joined the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> in 1995, and the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a> in 1999. Much of the late 1990s economic growth was fueled by the success of the mobile phone manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a>, which held a unique position of representing 80% of the market capitalization of the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Stock_Exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Helsinki Stock Exchange">Helsinki Stock Exchange</a>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Finland" title="Geography of Finland">Geography of Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_and_towns_in_Finland" title="List of cities and towns in Finland">List of cities and towns in Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_lakes_of_Finland" title="List of lakes of Finland">List of lakes of Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_parks_of_Finland" title="List of national parks of Finland">List of national parks of Finland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_in_Finland" title="Environmental issues in Finland">Environmental issues in Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Finland-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Map_of_Finland-en.svg/170px-Map_of_Finland-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Map_of_Finland-en.svg/255px-Map_of_Finland-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Map_of_Finland-en.svg/340px-Map_of_Finland-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1371" data-file-height="2007"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Finland-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Topographic map of Finland
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lying approximately between latitudes <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_north" title="60th parallel north">60°</a> and <a href="/wiki/70th_parallel_north" title="70th parallel north">70° N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/20th_meridian_east" title="20th meridian east">20°</a> and <a href="/wiki/32nd_meridian_east" title="32nd meridian east">32° E</a>, Finland is one of the world's northernmost countries. Of world capitals, only <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a> lies more to the north than Helsinki. The distance from the southernmost point – <a href="/wiki/Hanko,_Finland" title="Hanko, Finland">Hanko</a> in Uusimaa – to the northernmost – <a href="/wiki/Nuorgam" title="Nuorgam">Nuorgam</a> in Lapland – is 1,160 kilometres (720&nbsp;mi). </p>
