         </div>Supply and total consumption of electricity in Finland<sup id="cite_ref-Statistics_Finland,_Energy_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Statistics_Finland,_Energy-181">[180]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Finland has four privately owned nuclear reactors producing 18% of the country's energy<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> and one research reactor (decommissioned 2018 <sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup>) at the <a href="/wiki/Otaniemi" title="Otaniemi">Otaniemi</a> campus. The fifth <a href="/wiki/AREVA" class="mw-redirect" title="AREVA">AREVA</a>-<a href="/wiki/Siemens_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens AG">Siemens</a>-built reactor – the world's largest at 1600 <a href="/wiki/MWe" class="mw-redirect" title="MWe">MWe</a> and a focal point of Europe's nuclear industry – has faced many delays and is currently scheduled to be operational by 2018–2020, a decade after the original planned opening.<sup id="cite_ref-kauppalehti-02-2014_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kauppalehti-02-2014-184">[183]</a></sup> A varying amount (5–17%) of electricity has been imported from Russia (at around 3 gigawatt power line capacity), Sweden and Norway. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Onkalo_spent_nuclear_fuel_repository" title="Onkalo spent nuclear fuel repository">Onkalo spent nuclear fuel repository</a> is currently under construction at the <a href="/wiki/Olkiluoto_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Olkiluoto Nuclear Power Plant">Olkiluoto Nuclear Power Plant</a> in the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Eurajoki" title="Eurajoki">Eurajoki</a>, on the west coast of Finland, by the company <a href="/wiki/Posiva" title="Posiva">Posiva</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> Energy companies are about to increase nuclear power production, as in July 2010 the Finnish parliament granted permits for additional two new reactors. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Finland" title="Transport in Finland">Transport in Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:VR_Sr2_3202_Tampere_2012-06-22.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/VR_Sr2_3202_Tampere_2012-06-22.JPG/180px-VR_Sr2_3202_Tampere_2012-06-22.JPG" decoding="async" width="180" height="111" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/VR_Sr2_3202_Tampere_2012-06-22.JPG/270px-VR_Sr2_3202_Tampere_2012-06-22.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/VR_Sr2_3202_Tampere_2012-06-22.JPG/360px-VR_Sr2_3202_Tampere_2012-06-22.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3018" data-file-height="1862"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           A <a href="/wiki/VR_Class_Sr2" title="VR Class Sr2">VR Class Sr2</a> locomotive
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Vr_sr3_3304_3307_3306.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vr_sr3_3304_3307_3306.jpg/180px-Vr_sr3_3304_3307_3306.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="125" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vr_sr3_3304_3307_3306.jpg/270px-Vr_sr3_3304_3307_3306.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vr_sr3_3304_3307_3306.jpg/360px-Vr_sr3_3304_3307_3306.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2292" data-file-height="1590"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           Three <a href="/wiki/VR_Class_Sr3" title="VR Class Sr3">VR Class Sr3</a> locomotives
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:VR_Sr1_3089.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VR_Sr1_3089.jpg/180px-VR_Sr1_3089.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="114" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VR_Sr1_3089.jpg/270px-VR_Sr1_3089.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VR_Sr1_3089.jpg/360px-VR_Sr1_3089.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="508"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           Soviet-made electric locomotive <a href="/wiki/VR_Class_Sr1" title="VR Class Sr1">VR Class Sr1</a> model from 1981
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow" style="display:flex">
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          The state-owned <a href="/wiki/VR_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="VR (company)">VR</a> operates a railway network serving all major cities in Finland.
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Finland's road system is utilized by most internal cargo and passenger traffic. The annual state operated road network expenditure of around €1 billion is paid for with vehicle and fuel taxes which amount to around €1.5 billion and €1 billion, respectively. Among the <a href="/wiki/Highways_in_Finland" title="Highways in Finland">Finnish highways</a>, the most significant and busiest main roads include the <a href="/wiki/Turku_Highway" class="mw-redirect" title="Turku Highway">Turku Highway</a> (<a href="/wiki/European_route_E18" title="European route E18">E18</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Tampere_Highway" class="mw-redirect" title="Tampere Highway">Tampere Highway</a> (<a href="/wiki/European_route_E12" title="European route E12">E12</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Lahti_Highway" class="mw-redirect" title="Lahti Highway">Lahti Highway</a> (<a href="/wiki/European_route_E75" title="European route E75">E75</a>), and the ring roads (<a href="/wiki/Ring_I" title="Ring I">Ring I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ring_III" title="Ring III">Ring III</a>) of the Helsinki metropolitan area and the <a href="/wiki/Tampere_Ring_Road" title="Tampere Ring Road">Tampere Ring Road</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tampere_urban_area" title="Tampere urban area">Tampere urban area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The main international passenger gateway is <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Airport" title="Helsinki Airport">Helsinki Airport</a>, which handled about 17 million passengers in 2016. <a href="/wiki/Oulu_Airport" title="Oulu Airport">Oulu Airport</a> is the second largest, whilst another <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Finland" title="List of airports in Finland">25 airports</a> have scheduled passenger services.<sup id="cite_ref-finavia_pass_stats_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finavia_pass_stats-187">[186]</a></sup> The Helsinki Airport-based <a href="/wiki/Finnair" title="Finnair">Finnair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue1" title="Blue1">Blue1</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Regional_Airlines" title="Nordic Regional Airlines">Nordic Regional Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Air_Shuttle" title="Norwegian Air Shuttle">Norwegian Air Shuttle</a> sell air services both domestically and internationally. Helsinki has an optimal location for <a href="/wiki/Great_circle" title="Great circle">great circle</a> (i.e. the shortest and most efficient) routes between Western Europe and the Far East. </p>
      <p>Despite having a low population density, the Government annually spends around €350 million to maintain the 5,865-kilometre-long (3,644&nbsp;mi) network of railway tracks. Rail transport is handled by the state owned <a href="/wiki/VR_Group" title="VR Group">VR Group</a>, which has a 5% passenger market share (out of which 80% are from urban trips in Greater Helsinki) and 25% cargo market share.<sup id="cite_ref-ljvr_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ljvr-188">[187]</a></sup> Since 12 December 2010, <a href="/wiki/Karelian_Trains" title="Karelian Trains">Karelian Trains</a>, a joint venture between <a href="/wiki/Russian_Railways" title="Russian Railways">Russian Railways</a> and VR Group, has been running <a href="/wiki/Karelian_Trains_Class_Sm6" class="mw-redirect" title="Karelian Trains Class Sm6">Alstom Pendolino</a> operated high-speed services between Saint Petersburg's <a href="/wiki/Finlyandsky_Rail_Terminal" class="mw-redirect" title="Finlyandsky Rail Terminal">Finlyandsky</a> and Helsinki's <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Central_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Helsinki Central railway station">Central</a> railway stations. These services are branded as "Allegro" trains. The journey from Helsinki to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> takes only three and a half hours. A <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed rail</a> line is planned between <a href="/wiki/ELSA-rata" class="mw-redirect" title="ELSA-rata">Helsinki and Turku</a>, with a line from the capital to Tampere also proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> Helsinki opened the world's northernmost <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Metro" title="Helsinki Metro">metro system</a> in 1982, which also serves the neighbouring city of Espoo since 2017. </p>
      <p>The majority of international cargo shipments are handled at ports. <a href="/wiki/Vuosaari_Harbour" title="Vuosaari Harbour">Vuosaari Harbour</a> in Helsinki is the largest container port in Finland; others include <a href="/wiki/Kotka" title="Kotka">Kotka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamina" title="Hamina">Hamina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanko,_Finland" title="Hanko, Finland">Hanko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pori" title="Pori">Pori</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rauma,_Finland" title="Rauma, Finland">Rauma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oulu" title="Oulu">Oulu</a>. There is passenger traffic from Helsinki and Turku, which have ferry connections to <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mariehamn" title="Mariehamn">Mariehamn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Travem%C3%BCnde" title="Travemünde">Travemünde</a>. The Helsinki-Tallinn route – one of the busiest passenger sea routes in the world – has also been served by a helicopter line, and the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki-Tallinn_Tunnel" class="mw-redirect" title="Helsinki-Tallinn Tunnel">Helsinki-Tallinn Tunnel</a> has been proposed to provide railway services between the two cities.<sup id="cite_ref-busiest_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-busiest-190">[189]</a></sup> Largely following the example of the <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund_Bridge" title="Øresund Bridge">Øresund Bridge</a> between <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kvarken_Bridge" title="Kvarken Bridge">Kvarken Bridge</a> connecting <a href="/wiki/Ume%C3%A5" title="Umeå">Umeå</a> in Sweden and <a href="/wiki/Vaasa" title="Vaasa">Vaasa</a> in Finland to cross the Gulf of Bothnia has also been planned for decades.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Finland" title="Economy of Finland">Economy of Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Oasis_of_the_Seas.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Oasis_of_the_Seas.jpg/220px-Oasis_of_the_Seas.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Oasis_of_the_Seas.jpg/330px-Oasis_of_the_Seas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Oasis_of_the_Seas.jpg/440px-Oasis_of_the_Seas.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Oasis_of_the_Seas.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Oasis_of_the_Seas" title="Oasis of the Seas">Oasis of the Seas</a> was built at the <a href="/wiki/Perno_shipyard" title="Perno shipyard">Perno shipyard</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Finland rapidly industrialized after World War II, achieving GDP per capita levels comparable to that of Japan or the UK in the beginning of the 1970s. Initially, most of the economic development was based on two broad groups of export-led industries, the "metal industry" (<i>metalliteollisuus</i>) and "forest industry" (<i>metsäteollisuus</i>). The "metal industry" includes shipbuilding, metalworking, the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automotive industry</a>, engineered products such as motors and <a href="/wiki/Electronics_industry" title="Electronics industry">electronics</a>, and production of metals and alloys including <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>. Many of the world's biggest <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ships</a>, including <a href="/wiki/MS_Freedom_of_the_Seas" title="MS Freedom of the Seas">MS Freedom of the Seas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Oasis_of_the_Seas" title="Oasis of the Seas">Oasis of the Seas</a> have been built in Finnish shipyards.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup> The "forest industry" includes forestry, timber, pulp and paper, and is often considered a logical development based on Finland's extensive forest resources, as 73% of the area is covered by forest. In the <a href="/wiki/Pulp_and_paper_industry" title="Pulp and paper industry">pulp and paper industry</a>, many major companies are based in Finland; <a href="/wiki/Ahlstrom-Munksj%C3%B6" title="Ahlstrom-Munksjö">Ahlstrom-Munksjö</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mets%C3%A4_Board" title="Metsä Board">Metsä Board</a>, and <a href="/wiki/UPM_(company)" title="UPM (company)">UPM</a> are all Finnish forest-based companies with revenues exceeding €1 billion. However, in recent decades, the Finnish economy has diversified, with companies expanding into fields such as electronics (<a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a>), metrology (<a href="/wiki/Vaisala" title="Vaisala">Vaisala</a>), petroleum (<a href="/wiki/Neste" title="Neste">Neste</a>), and video games (<a href="/wiki/Rovio_Entertainment" title="Rovio Entertainment">Rovio Entertainment</a>), and is no longer dominated by the two sectors of metal and forest industry. Likewise, the structure has changed, with the service sector growing, with manufacturing declining in importance; agriculture remains a minor part. Despite this, production for export is still more prominent than in Western Europe, thus making Finland possibly more vulnerable to global economic trends. </p>
      <p>In 2017, the Finnish economy was estimated to consist of approximately 2.7% agriculture, 28.2% manufacturing and 69.1% services.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup> In 2019, the per-capita income of Finland was estimated to be $48,869. In 2020, Finland was ranked 20th on the <a href="/wiki/Ease_of_doing_business_index" title="Ease of doing business index">ease of doing business index</a>, among 190 jurisdictions. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_policy">Public policy</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Nordic_model" title="Nordic model">Nordic model</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nordiske-flag.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Nordiske-flag.jpg/220px-Nordiske-flag.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Nordiske-flag.jpg/330px-Nordiske-flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Nordiske-flag.jpg/440px-Nordiske-flag.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="562"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nordiske-flag.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Flags of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a> from left to right: Finland, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Finnish politicians have often emulated the Nordic model.<sup id="cite_ref-nordicmodel_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nordicmodel-195">[194]</a></sup> Nordics have been free-trading and relatively welcoming to skilled migrants for over a century, though in Finland <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> is relatively new. The level of protection in commodity trade has been low, except for agricultural products.<sup id="cite_ref-nordicmodel_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nordicmodel-195">[194]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Finland has top levels of economic freedom in many areas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Finland is ranked 16th in the 2008 global <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Economic_Freedom" title="Index of Economic Freedom">Index of Economic Freedom</a> and 9th in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-freedom_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedom-196">[195]</a></sup> While the manufacturing sector is thriving, the OECD points out that the service sector would benefit substantially from policy improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The 2007 <a href="/wiki/International_Institute_for_Management_Development" title="International Institute for Management Development">IMD</a> World Competitiveness Yearbook ranked Finland 17th most <a href="/wiki/Competitiveness" class="mw-redirect" title="Competitiveness">competitive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> 2008 index ranked Finland the 6th most competitive.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup> In both indicators, Finland's performance was next to Germany, and significantly higher than most European countries. In the Business competitiveness index 2007–2008 Finland ranked third in the world. </p>
      <p>Economists attribute much growth to reforms in the product markets. According to the OECD, only four <a href="/wiki/EU-15" class="mw-redirect" title="EU-15">EU-15</a> countries have less regulated <a href="/wiki/Product_market" title="Product market">product markets</a> (UK, Ireland, Denmark and Sweden) and only one has less regulated <a href="/wiki/Financial_market" title="Financial market">financial markets</a> (Denmark). Nordic countries were pioneers in liberalizing energy, postal, and other markets in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-nordicmodel_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nordicmodel-195">[194]</a></sup> The legal system is clear and business bureaucracy less than most countries.<sup id="cite_ref-freedom_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedom-196">[195]</a></sup> Property rights are well protected and contractual agreements are strictly honoured.<sup id="cite_ref-freedom_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedom-196">[195]</a></sup> Finland is rated the least corrupt country in the world in the <a href="/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index" title="Corruption Perceptions Index">Corruption Perceptions Index</a><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup> and 13th in the <a href="/wiki/Ease_of_doing_business_index" title="Ease of doing business index">Ease of doing business index</a>. This indicates exceptional ease in cross-border trading (5th), contract enforcement (7th), business closure (5th), tax payment (83rd), and low worker hardship (127th).<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Finnish law <a href="/wiki/Universal_validity_of_collective_labour_agreements" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal validity of collective labour agreements">forces all workers to obey the national contracts</a> that are drafted every few years for each profession and seniority level. The agreement becomes universally enforceable provided that more than 50% of the employees support it, in practice by being a member of a relevant trade union. The unionization rate is high (70%), especially in the middle class (<a href="/wiki/AKAVA" class="mw-redirect" title="AKAVA">AKAVA</a>—80%). A lack of a national agreement in an industry is considered an exception.<sup id="cite_ref-oecd2004_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oecd2004-155">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nordicmodel_195-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nordicmodel-195">[194]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Finland" title="Tourism in Finland">Tourism in Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Porvoon_tuomiokirkko_N%C3%A4sinm%C3%A4elt%C3%A4.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Porvoon_tuomiokirkko_N%C3%A4sinm%C3%A4elt%C3%A4.JPG/220px-Porvoon_tuomiokirkko_N%C3%A4sinm%C3%A4elt%C3%A4.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Porvoon_tuomiokirkko_N%C3%A4sinm%C3%A4elt%C3%A4.JPG/330px-Porvoon_tuomiokirkko_N%C3%A4sinm%C3%A4elt%C3%A4.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Porvoon_tuomiokirkko_N%C3%A4sinm%C3%A4elt%C3%A4.JPG/440px-Porvoon_tuomiokirkko_N%C3%A4sinm%C3%A4elt%C3%A4.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5051" data-file-height="3351"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Porvoon_tuomiokirkko_N%C3%A4sinm%C3%A4elt%C3%A4.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">Medieval</a> old town in <a href="/wiki/Porvoo" title="Porvoo">Porvoo</a> is one of the most popular tourist destinations in summers for those who are fascinated by the old look.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hameenlinna.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hameenlinna.JPG/220px-Hameenlinna.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hameenlinna.JPG/330px-Hameenlinna.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hameenlinna.JPG/440px-Hameenlinna.JPG 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hameenlinna.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The historical <a href="/wiki/Tavastia_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Tavastia Castle">Tavastia Castle</a> (or Häme Castle) in <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4meenlinna" title="Hämeenlinna">Hämeenlinna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tavastia_Proper" class="mw-redirect" title="Tavastia Proper">Tavastia Proper</a> is located close to the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Vanajavesi" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake Vanajavesi">Lake Vanajavesi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2017, tourism in Finland grossed approximately €15.0 billion with a 7% increase from the previous year. Of this, €4.6 billion (30%) came from foreign tourism.<sup id="cite_ref-Matkailutilinpito_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matkailutilinpito-206">[205]</a></sup> In 2017, there were 15.2 million overnight stays of domestic tourists and 6.7 million overnight stays of foreign tourists.<sup id="cite_ref-TEM_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TEM-207">[206]</a></sup> Much of the sudden growth can be attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Globalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Globalisation">globalisation</a> of the country as well as a rise in positive publicity and awareness. While Russia remains the largest market for foreign tourists, the biggest growth came from Chinese markets (35%).<sup id="cite_ref-TEM_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TEM-207">[206]</a></sup> Tourism contributes roughly 2.7% to Finland's GDP, making it comparable to agriculture and forestry.<sup id="cite_ref-Tourism_infographic_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tourism_infographic-208">[207]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea_cruiseferries" title="Baltic Sea cruiseferries">Commercial cruises</a> between major coastal and port cities in the Baltic region, including Helsinki, <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mariehamn" title="Mariehamn">Mariehamn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Travem%C3%BCnde" title="Travemünde">Travemünde</a>, play a significant role in the local tourism industry. There are also separate ferry connections dedicated to tourism in the vicinity of Helsinki and its region, such as the connection to the fortress island of <a href="/wiki/Suomenlinna" title="Suomenlinna">Suomenlinna</a><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> or the connection to the old town of <a href="/wiki/Porvoo" title="Porvoo">Porvoo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup> By passenger counts, the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Helsinki" title="Port of Helsinki">Port of Helsinki</a> is the <a href="/wiki/World%27s_busiest_ports" title="World's busiest ports">busiest port in the world</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Dover" title="Port of Dover">Port of Dover</a> in the United Kingdom and the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Tallinn" title="Port of Tallinn">Port of Tallinn</a> in Estonia.<sup id="cite_ref-POH_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-POH-211">[210]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Helsinki-Vantaa_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Helsinki-Vantaa International Airport">Helsinki-Vantaa International Airport</a> is the fourth busiest airport in the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a> in terms of passenger numbers,<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup> and about 90% of Finland's international air traffic passes through the airport.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">Lapland</a> has the highest tourism consumption of any Finnish region.<sup id="cite_ref-Tourism_infographic_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tourism_infographic-208">[207]</a></sup> Above the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>, in midwinter, there is a <a href="/wiki/Polar_night" title="Polar night">polar night</a>, a period when the sun does not rise for days or weeks, or even months, and correspondingly, <a href="/wiki/Midnight_sun" title="Midnight sun">midnight sun</a> in the summer, with no sunset even at midnight (for up to 73 consecutive days, at the northernmost point). Lapland is so far north that the <a href="/wiki/Aurora_borealis" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurora borealis">aurora borealis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a> in the high atmosphere due to <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, is seen regularly in the fall, winter, and spring. Finnish Lapland is also locally regarded as the home of Saint Nicholas or <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a>, with several theme parks, such as <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus_Village" title="Santa Claus Village">Santa Claus Village</a> and <a href="/wiki/Santa_Park" title="Santa Park">Santa Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rovaniemi" title="Rovaniemi">Rovaniemi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup> Other significant tourist destinations in Lapland also include <a href="/wiki/Ski_resort" title="Ski resort">ski resorts</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Yll%C3%A4s" title="Ylläs">Ylläs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levi,_Finland" title="Levi, Finland">Levi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruka,_Finland" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruka, Finland">Ruka</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sleigh" class="mw-redirect" title="Sleigh">sleigh</a> rides led by either <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a> or <a href="/wiki/Husky" title="Husky">huskies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Tourist attractions in Finland include the natural landscape found throughout the country as well as urban attractions. Finland is covered with thick <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> forests, rolling hills, and lakes. Finland contains 40 <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_parks_of_Finland" title="List of national parks of Finland">national parks</a> (such as the <a href="/wiki/Koli_National_Park" title="Koli National Park">Koli National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Karelia" title="North Karelia">North Karelia</a>), from the Southern shores of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a> to the high <a href="/wiki/Fell" title="Fell">fells</a> of Lapland. Outdoor activities range from <a href="/wiki/Nordic_skiing" title="Nordic skiing">Nordic skiing</a>, golf, fishing, <a href="/wiki/Yachting" title="Yachting">yachting</a>, lake cruises, hiking, and <a href="/wiki/Kayaking" title="Kayaking">kayaking</a>, among many others. <a href="/wiki/Bird-watching" class="mw-redirect" title="Bird-watching">Bird-watching</a> is popular for those fond of avifauna, however hunting is also popular. <a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">Elk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hare</a> are common game in Finland. </p>
      <p>Finland also has urbanised regions with many cultural events and activities. The most famous <a href="/wiki/List_of_tourist_attractions_in_Helsinki" title="List of tourist attractions in Helsinki">tourist attractions in Helsinki</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Cathedral" title="Helsinki Cathedral">Helsinki Cathedral</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Suomenlinna" title="Suomenlinna">Suomenlinna</a> sea fortress. The most well-known Finnish amusement parks include <a href="/wiki/Linnanm%C3%A4ki" title="Linnanmäki">Linnanmäki</a> in Helsinki, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A4rk%C3%A4nniemi" title="Särkänniemi">Särkänniemi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tampere" title="Tampere">Tampere</a>, <a href="/wiki/PowerPark" title="PowerPark">PowerPark</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kauhava" title="Kauhava">Kauhava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tykkim%C3%A4ki" title="Tykkimäki">Tykkimäki</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kouvola" title="Kouvola">Kouvola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nokkakivi" title="Nokkakivi">Nokkakivi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Laukaa" title="Laukaa">Laukaa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[217]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/St._Olaf%27s_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Olaf's Castle">St. Olaf's Castle</a> (<i>Olavinlinna</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Savonlinna" title="Savonlinna">Savonlinna</a> hosts the annual <a href="/wiki/Savonlinna_Opera_Festival" title="Savonlinna Opera Festival">Savonlinna Opera Festival</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> milieus of the cities of <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rauma,_Finland" title="Rauma, Finland">Rauma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porvoo" title="Porvoo">Porvoo</a> also attract curious spectators.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[219]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Finland" title="Demographics of Finland">Demographics of Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px"> 
        <div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black"> 
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:#fb9a99"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 4300.7099943192px; border-left-color:#ffff00"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#ffff00"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#ffff00"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 1250.5275167023px; border-left-color:#ff471a"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#ff471a"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#ff471a"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 414.23608853816px; border-left-color:#4080bf"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#4080bf"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#4080bf"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 165.04160635584px; border-left-color:#003399"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#003399"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#003399"></div> 
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption"> 
         <p>Population by ethnic background in 2017<sup id="cite_ref-auto_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-un_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-un-2">[2]</a></sup> </p> 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#003399; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Finnish (91.33%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#4080bf; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Other European (4.90%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#ff471a; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Asian (2.50%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#ffff00; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;African (0.90%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#fb9a99; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Other (0.37%)
         </div> 
        </div> 
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The population of Finland is currently about 5.5 million. The current <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rate</a> is 10.42 per 1,000 residents, for a <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">fertility rate</a> of 1.49 children born per woman,<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup> one of the lowest in the world, significantly below the replacement rate of 2.1. In 1887 Finland recorded its highest rate, 5.17 children born per woman.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[221]</a></sup> Finland has one of the oldest populations in the world, with a median age of 42.6 years.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[222]</a></sup> Approximately half of voters are estimated to be over 50 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-populationdevelopment_86-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-populationdevelopment-86">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pop_stat_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop_stat-226">[225]</a></sup> Finland has an average population density of 18 inhabitants per square kilometre. This is the third-lowest population density of any European country, behind those of Norway and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, and the lowest population density of any European Union member country. Finland's population has always been concentrated in the southern parts of the country, a phenomenon that became even more pronounced during 20th-century urbanisation. Two of the three largest cities in Finland are situated in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Helsinki" title="Greater Helsinki">Greater Helsinki</a> <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">metropolitan area</a>—Helsinki and <a href="/wiki/Espoo" title="Espoo">Espoo</a>, and some municipalities in the metropolitan area have also shown clear growth of population year after year, the most notable being <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A4rvenp%C3%A4%C3%A4" title="Järvenpää">Järvenpää</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nurmij%C3%A4rvi" title="Nurmijärvi">Nurmijärvi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirkkonummi" title="Kirkkonummi">Kirkkonummi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerava" title="Kerava">Kerava</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sipoo" title="Sipoo">Sipoo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[226]</a></sup> In the largest cities of Finland, <a href="/wiki/Tampere" title="Tampere">Tampere</a> holds the third place after Helsinki and Espoo while also Helsinki-neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Vantaa" title="Vantaa">Vantaa</a> is the fourth. Other cities with population over 100,000 are <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oulu" title="Oulu">Oulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jyv%C3%A4skyl%C3%A4" title="Jyväskylä">Jyväskylä</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuopio" title="Kuopio">Kuopio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lahti" title="Lahti">Lahti</a>. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Sottunga" title="Sottunga">Sottunga</a> of the <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">Åland Islands</a> is the smallest municipality in Finland in terms of population (<a href="/wiki/Luhanka" title="Luhanka">Luhanka</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Finland" title="Mainland Finland">mainland Finland</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[227]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Savukoski" title="Savukoski">Savukoski</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">Lapland</a> is sparsely populated in terms of <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">population density</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[228]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there were 423,494 people with a foreign background living in Finland (7.7% of the population), most of whom are from the former Soviet Union, Estonia, Somalia, Iraq and former Yugoslavia.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[230]</a></sup> The children of foreigners are not automatically given Finnish citizenship, as Finnish nationality law practices and maintain <i><a href="/wiki/Jus_sanguinis" title="Jus sanguinis">jus sanguinis</a></i> policy where only children born to at least one Finnish parent are granted citizenship. If they are born in Finland and cannot get citizenship of any other country, they become citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[231]</a></sup> Additionally, certain persons of Finnish descent who reside in countries that were once part of <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, retain the <a href="/wiki/Right_of_return#Finland" title="Right of return">right of return</a>, a right to establish permanent residency in the country, which would eventually entitle them to qualify for citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[232]</a></sup> 387,215 people in Finland in 2018 were born in another country, representing 7% of the population. The 10 largest foreign born groups are (in order) from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[233]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Finland's immigrant population is growing. By 2035, the three largest cites in Finland are projected to have over a quarter of residents of a foreign-speaking background: in Helsinki, they are projected to form 26% of the population; in <a href="/wiki/Espoo" title="Espoo">Espoo</a>, 30%; and in <a href="/wiki/Vantaa" title="Vantaa">Vantaa</a>, 34%. The Helsinki region is projected to have 437,000 people of a foreign linguistic background, compared to 201,000 in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="***REMOVED***">Language</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland_Swedish" title="Finland Swedish">Finland Swedish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Finland" title="Languages of Finland">Languages of Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_municipalities_of_Finland_in_which_Finnish_is_not_the_sole_official_language" title="List of municipalities of Finland in which Finnish is not the sole official language">List of municipalities of Finland in which Finnish is not the sole official language</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Languages_of_Finnish_municipalities_(2016).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Languages_of_Finnish_municipalities_%282016%29.svg/220px-Languages_of_Finnish_municipalities_%282016%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="383" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Languages_of_Finnish_municipalities_%282016%29.svg/330px-Languages_of_Finnish_municipalities_%282016%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Languages_of_Finnish_municipalities_%282016%29.svg/440px-Languages_of_Finnish_municipalities_%282016%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1850" data-file-height="3220"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Languages_of_Finnish_municipalities_(2016).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Municipalities of Finland: 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#EEEEC1; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;unilingually Finnish
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#37ABC8; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;bilingual with Finnish as majority language, Swedish as minority language
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#0055D4; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;bilingual with Swedish as majority language, Finnish as minority language
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#000080; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;unilingually Swedish
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#800033; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;bilingual with Finnish as majority language, Sami as minority language
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Finnish and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a> are the official languages of Finland. Finnish predominates nationwide while Swedish is spoken in some coastal areas in the west and south (such as <a href="/wiki/Raseborg" title="Raseborg">Raseborg</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[235]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pargas" title="Pargas">Pargas</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-237">[236]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/N%C3%A4rpes" title="Närpes">Närpes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-237">[236]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kristinestad" title="Kristinestad">Kristinestad</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[237]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jakobstad" title="Jakobstad">Jakobstad</a><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[238]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nykarleby" title="Nykarleby">Nykarleby</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[239]</a></sup>) and in the autonomous region of <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land" class="mw-redirect" title="Åland">Åland</a>, which is the only monolingual Swedish-speaking region in Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[240]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Native_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Native language">native language</a> of 87.3% of the population is Finnish,<sup id="cite_ref-Population_according_to_language_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Population_according_to_language-242">[241]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[242]</a></sup> which is part of the Finnic subgroup of the Uralic languages. The language is one of only four official <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">EU languages</a> not of <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> origin, and has no relation to the other <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">national languages</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordics</a>. Conversely, Finnish is closely related to <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karelian_language" title="Karelian language">Karelian</a>, and more distantly to <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> and the Sami languages. </p>
      <p>Swedish is the native language of 5.2% of the population (<a href="/wiki/Swedish-speaking_Finns" class="mw-redirect" title="Swedish-speaking Finns">Swedish-speaking Finns</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-population-2016_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-population-2016-244">[243]</a></sup> Finnish is dominant in all the country's larger cities; though Helsinki, <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaasa" title="Vaasa">Vaasa</a> were once predominantly Swedish-speaking, they have undergone a language shift since the 19th century to become largely Finnish-speaking. </p>
      <p>Swedish is a compulsory school subject and general knowledge of the language is good among non-native speakers. In 2005, a total of 47% of Finnish citizens reported the ability to speak Swedish, either as primary or a secondary language.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[244]</a></sup> Likewise, a majority of mainland Sweden Finns are able to speak Finnish. However, most Sweden Finnish youth reported seldom using Finnish: 71% reported always or mostly speaking Swedish in social settings outside of their households.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[245]</a></sup> The Finnish side of the land border with Sweden is unilingually Finnish-speaking, with a stark language divide over the <a href="/wiki/Torne_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Torne River">Torne River</a>: on the Swedish side, a Northern Swedish accent can be heard that is distinct from the Swedish spoken in other parts of Finland. There is a sizeable pronunciation difference between the varieties of Swedish spoken in the two countries, although their <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a> is nearly universal.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[246]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries#Languages" title="Nordic countries">Nordic</a> languages and <a href="/wiki/Karelian_language" title="Karelian language">Karelian</a> are also specially recognized in parts of Finland. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Finnish_Kalo_language" title="Finnish Kalo language">Finnish Romani</a> is spoken by some 5,000–6,000 people; Romani and <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Sign_Language" title="Finnish Sign Language">Finnish Sign Language</a> are also recognized in the constitution. There are two sign languages: Finnish Sign Language, spoken natively by 4,000–5,000 people,<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[247]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Finland-Swedish_Sign_Language" title="Finland-Swedish Sign Language">Finland-Swedish Sign Language</a>, spoken natively by about 150 people. <a href="/wiki/Mishar_Tatar_dialect" title="Mishar Tatar dialect">Tatar</a> is spoken by a <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Tatars" title="Finnish Tatars">Finnish Tatar</a> minority of about 800 people whose ancestors moved to Finland mainly during Russian rule from the 1870s to the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[248]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">Sami language</a> has an official status in Lapland where the Sami people predominate, numbering around 7,000<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[249]</a></sup> and recognized as an <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous people</a>. About a quarter of them speak a Sami language as their mother tongue.<sup id="cite_ref-Population_2006-12-31_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Population_2006-12-31-251">[250]</a></sup> The Sami languages that are spoken in Finland are <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sami" title="Northern Sami">Northern Sami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inari_Sami_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Inari Sami language">Inari Sami</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Skolt_Sami_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Skolt Sami language">Skolt Sami</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[note 2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The rights of minority groups (in particular <a href="/wiki/Sami_people#Finland" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami people">Sami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swedish-speaking_population_of_Finland" title="Swedish-speaking population of Finland">Swedish speakers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Kale" title="Finnish Kale">Romani people</a>) are protected by the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The largest immigrant languages are <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> (1.5%), <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> (0.9%), <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> (0.6%), <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a> (0.4%) and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> (0.4%).<sup id="cite_ref-population-2016_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-population-2016-244">[243]</a></sup> English is studied by most pupils as a compulsory subject from the first grade (at seven years of age) in the comprehensive school (in some schools other languages can be chosen instead),<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup> as a result of which <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">Finns</a>' English language skills have been significantly strengthened over several decades.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup> German, French, Spanish and Russian can be studied as second foreign languages from the fourth grade (at 10 years of age; some schools may offer other options).<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[256]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>About 93% of Finns can speak a second language.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup> The figures in this section should be treated with caution, as they come from the official Finnish population register. People can only register one language and so bilingual or multilingual language users' language competencies are not properly included. A citizen of Finland that speaks bilingually Finnish and Swedish will often be registered as a Finnish only speaker in this system. Similarly "old domestic language" is a category applied to some languages and not others for political not linguistic reasons, for example Russian.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup> </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Finland" title="Religion in Finland">Religion in Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px"> 
        <div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black"> 
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:Honeydew"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:97.605363273241px 21.753001179118px 0 0; border-top-color:Chartreuse"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Chartreuse"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:96.45574184578px 26.387304996537px 0 0; border-top-color:DarkOrchid"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:DarkOrchid"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:94.750086672456px 31.975319788268px 0 0; border-top-color:Orchid"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Orchid"></div>
         <div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:92.315655036448px 38.442422337718px 0 0; border-top-color:DodgerBlue"></div> 
         <div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:DodgerBlue"></div> 
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption"> 
         <p>Religions in Finland (2019)<sup id="cite_ref-stnin_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stnin-3">[3]</a></sup> </p> 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:DodgerBlue; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland (68.72%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:Orchid; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Orthodox Church (1.10%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:DarkOrchid; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Other Christian (0.93%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:Chartreuse; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Other religions (0.76%)
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:Honeydew; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Unaffiliated (28.49%)
         </div> 
        </div> 
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>With 3.9 million members,<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_of_Finland" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland">Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland</a> is one of the largest Lutheran churches in the world and is also by far Finland's largest religious body; at the end of 2019, 68.7% of Finns were members of the church.<sup id="cite_ref-relig_stat_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-relig_stat-262">[260]</a></sup> The Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland has seen its share of the country's population declining by roughly one percent annually in recent years.<sup id="cite_ref-relig_stat_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-relig_stat-262">[260]</a></sup> The decline has been due to both church membership resignations and falling baptism rates.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup> The second largest group, accounting for 26.3% of the population<sup id="cite_ref-relig_stat_262-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-relig_stat-262">[260]</a></sup> in 2017, has no religious affiliation. The irreligious group rose quickly from just below 13% in the year 2000. A small minority belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnish Orthodox Church">Finnish Orthodox Church</a> (1.1%). Other <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> denominations and the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_Finland" title="Catholic Church in Finland">Roman Catholic Church</a> are significantly smaller, as are the <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> and other non-Christian communities (totalling 1.6%); for example, in the Protestant trend, there are about 1,500 <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a> concentrated in the region of <a href="/wiki/Central_Finland" title="Central Finland">Central Finland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup> and there are only about 2,000 <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodists</a> who are scattered around the country.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> estimated the Muslim population at 2.7% in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[265]</a></sup> The main Lutheran and Orthodox churches are <a href="/wiki/National_church" title="National church">national churches</a> of Finland with special roles such as in state ceremonies and schools.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1869, Finland was the first Nordic country to <a href="/wiki/Disestablishment" class="mw-redirect" title="Disestablishment">disestablish</a> its Evangelical Lutheran church by introducing the Church Act, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Sweden" title="Church of Sweden">Church of Sweden</a> in 2000. Although the church still maintains a special relationship with the state, it is not described as a <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Finland" title="Constitution of Finland">Finnish Constitution</a> or other laws passed by the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnish Parliament">Finnish Parliament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FinlandConstitution_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinlandConstitution-269">[267]</a></sup> Finland's state church was the Church of Sweden until 1809. As an autonomous Grand Duchy under Russia 1809–1917, Finland retained the Lutheran State Church system, and a state church separate from Sweden, later named the Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland, was established. It was detached from the state as a separate judicial entity when the new church law came to force in 1869. After Finland had gained independence in 1917, religious freedom was declared in the constitution of 1919 and a separate law on religious freedom in 1922. Through this arrangement, the Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland lost its position as a state church but gained a constitutional status as a national church alongside the Finnish Orthodox Church, whose position however is not codified in the constitution. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki,_Finlandia,_2012-08-14,_DD_01.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG/220px-Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG/330px-Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG/440px-Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4012" data-file-height="3448"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki,_Finlandia,_2012-08-14,_DD_01.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Evangelical Lutheran <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Cathedral" title="Helsinki Cathedral">Helsinki Cathedral</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2016, 69.3% of Finnish children were <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptized</a><sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[268]</a></sup> and 82.3% were <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Lutheran_Church)" title="Confirmation (Lutheran Church)">confirmed</a> in 2012 at the age of 15,<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup> and over 90% of the funerals are Christian. However, the majority of Lutherans attend church only for special occasions like Christmas ceremonies, weddings, and funerals. The Lutheran Church estimates that approximately 1.8% of its members attend church services weekly.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[270]</a></sup> The average number of church visits per year by church members is approximately two.<sup id="cite_ref-state2004_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state2004-273">[271]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to a 2010 <a href="/wiki/Eurobarometer" title="Eurobarometer">Eurobarometer</a> poll, 33% of Finnish citizens responded that they "believe there is a God"; 42% answered that they "believe there is some sort of spirit or life force"; and 22% that they "do not believe there is any sort of spirit, God, or life force".<sup id="cite_ref-EUROBAROMETER_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUROBAROMETER-274">[272]</a></sup> According to ISSP survey data (2008), 8% consider themselves "highly religious", and 31% "moderately religious". In the same survey, 28% reported themselves as "agnostic" and 29% as "non-religious".<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[273]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Finland" title="Healthcare in Finland">Healthcare in Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Meilahdensairaala2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Meilahdensairaala2.jpg/220px-Meilahdensairaala2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Meilahdensairaala2.jpg/330px-Meilahdensairaala2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Meilahdensairaala2.jpg/440px-Meilahdensairaala2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2037" data-file-height="1443"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Meilahdensairaala2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Meilahti_Tower_Hospital" title="Meilahti Tower Hospital">Meilahti Tower Hospital</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_University_Central_Hospital" title="Helsinki University Central Hospital">Helsinki University Central Hospital</a> (HUCH) in <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B6%C3%B6l%C3%B6" title="Töölö">Töölö</a>, Helsinki
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">Life expectancy</a> has increased from 71 years for men and 79 years for women in 1990 to 79 years for men and 84 years for women in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[274]</a></sup> The under-five mortality rate has decreased from 51 per 1,000 live births in 1950 to 2.3 per 1,000 live births in 2017, ranking Finland's rate among the lowest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[275]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertility rate">fertility rate</a> in 2014 stood at 1.71 children born/per woman and has been below <a href="/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility" title="Sub-replacement fertility">the replacement rate</a> of 2.1 since 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-stat.fi_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat.fi-278">[276]</a></sup> With a low birth rate women also become mothers at a later age, the mean age at first live birth being 28.6 in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-stat.fi_278-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat.fi-278">[276]</a></sup> A 2011 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">The Lancet</a></i> medical journal found that Finland had the lowest <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillbirth</a> rate out of 193 countries, including the UK, France and New Zealand.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[277]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There has been a slight increase or no change in welfare and health inequalities between population groups in the 21st century. Lifestyle-related diseases are on the rise. More than half a million Finns suffer from <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Type_1_diabetes" title="Type 1 diabetes">type 1 diabetes</a> being globally the most common in Finland. Many children are diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>. The number of <a href="/wiki/Musculoskeletal_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Musculoskeletal disease">musculoskeletal diseases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancers</a> are increasing, although the cancer prognosis has improved. Allergies and dementia are also growing health problems in Finland. One of the most common reasons for work disability are due to mental disorders, in particular <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup> Treatment for depression has improved and as a result the historically high suicide rates have declined to 13 per 100 000 in 2017, closer to the North European average.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[279]</a></sup> Suicide rates are still among the highest among developed countries in the OECD.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are 307 residents for each doctor.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup> About 19% of health care is funded directly by households and 77% by taxation. </p>
      <p>In April 2012, Finland was ranked 2nd in Gross National Happiness in a report published by The Earth Institute.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[282]</a></sup> Since 2012, Finland has every time ranked at least in the top 5 of world's happiest countries in the annual <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report</a> by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[283]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[285]</a></sup> as well as ranking as the happiest country in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_science">Education and science</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Finland" title="Education in Finland">Education in Finland</a>
      </div>

      </div>
